<assistant>:
Dear ___,

You presented to the hospital on March 1, 2024, with complaints of severe chest pain radiating to your left arm and shortness of breath. Upon admission, an electrocardiogram (ECG) revealed an ST-elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) indicative of an acute transmural myocardial infarction of the anterior wall.

Immediate treatment included a percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI), during which a stent was placed successfully. Post-procedure, your condition stabilized, and no recurrent chest pain was observed. Continuous monitoring and management were maintained.

Throughout your stay, a range of medications were administered, including Aspirin (325 mg once daily), Clopidogrel (75 mg once daily), and a continuous infusion of IV Heparin adjusted as per protocol. Furthermore, Nitroglycerin was prescribed as needed for chest pain, and from March 2, a regime including Metoprolol (50 mg twice daily), Lisinopril (10 mg once daily), and a Statin (40 mg once daily) was initiated.

Your laboratory investigations showed elevated troponin levels, peaking 12 hours post-admission, consistent with myocardial infarction. Subsequent tests on March 2 and 3 demonstrated a decrease in troponin, indicating a positive response to treatment. Other tests, including CBC, were within normal limits, though your lipid profile did indicate elevated LDL cholesterol, which aligns with your diagnosis and treatment plan.

During your hospitalization, daily ward rounds confirmed stabilization of your condition with no complications noted. By March 3, you reported symptomatic improvement, and by March 4, significant recovery was seen, allowing us to discuss discharge plans and rehabilitation strategies. We ensured comprehensive education concerning your medication regimen and lifestyle modifications, including dietary and exercise recommendations.

You are advised to follow a heart-healthy diet low in saturated fats, cholesterol, and sodium, and gradually reintroduce physical activities, starting with light walking. Additionally, immediate smoking cessation is essential, supported by nicotine replacement therapy and counseling if needed. Stress management techniques such as mindfulness or yoga are also recommended.

A follow-up appointment has been scheduled at the cardiology outpatient clinic on March 20, 2024, to assess your recovery and manage ongoing medication. It is crucial to comply with the medication and lifestyle modifications as discussed, to support your recovery process and prevent future cardiac events.

We trust this plan will aid in your recovery, and we are here to support you during this rehabilitation period. We wish you a speedy and complete recovery.

Sincerely,

___

<user>
The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

This is an example of discharge summary letter:
Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

<assistant>:
Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 1, 2024, due to severe chest pain radiating to your left arm and accompanied by shortness of breath. An electrocardiogram (ECG) conducted upon admission revealed an ST-elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) of the anterior wall. Consequently, a diagnosis of acute transmural myocardial infarction of the anterior wall was confirmed. Immediate intervention included a percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) with stent placement. Following the procedure, you remained stable.

During your hospitalization, you received several medications to manage your condition effectively. On March 1, 2024, you were started on Aspirin 325 mg once daily, Clopidogrel 75 mg once daily, and continuous infusion of IV Heparin, adjusted per protocol. Nitroglycerin was administered at 0.4 mg every 5 minutes as needed for chest pain. Subsequently, on March 2, 2024, Metoprolol, a beta-blocker (50 mg twice daily), Lisinopril, an ACE inhibitor (10 mg once daily), and a statin (40 mg once daily) were added to your regimen.

Throughout your stay, your condition showed marked improvement. By March 2, you reported feeling better, and your troponin levels, initially elevated, began to decrease. This trend continued through March 3, confirming your stable condition and planned rehabilitation. Discharge planning included educating you on your medication regimen, which you seemed to comprehend well.

On March 4, your pre-discharge checks were conducted successfully, and you were reported ready for discharge. By March 5, all discharge processes, including final checks and patient education, were completed.

At discharge on March 6, 2024, your condition is stable with no recurrent chest pain, and all vital signs are within normal limits. Troponin levels have continued to decrease, indicating recovery from the myocardial infarction.

For your ongoing care, please adhere to the medication regimen as prescribed at discharge. You have a scheduled follow-up appointment at the Cardiology outpatient clinic on March 20, 2024, to evaluate your recovery and ongoing medication management.

It is essential to adopt certain lifestyle modifications for your heart health. Consume a heart-healthy diet low in saturated fats, cholesterol, and sodium while increasing your intake of fruits, vegetables, and whole grains. Gradually reintroduce physical activity, starting with light walking, and aim to achieve at least 150 minutes of moderate-intensity aerobic activity each week. Immediate smoking cessation is strongly recommended, and you may consider nicotine replacement therapy and counseling for support. Additionally, engaging in stress management techniques such as mindfulness, yoga, or meditation may be beneficial.

We wish you a smooth recovery and look forward to seeing you at your follow-up appointment.

Sincerely,

___

<user>
The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

This is an example of discharge summary letter:
Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 1, 2024, due to severe chest pain radiating to your left arm and shortness of breath. Through diagnostic evaluations, including an ECG, you were identified with ST-elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) of the anterior wall, recorded as an acute transmural myocardial infarction. In response to this, a Percutaneous Coronary Intervention (PCI) was performed, during which a stent was successfully placed to address the blockage.

Following the procedure, you demonstrated stability, with no recurrent chest pain and stable vital signs. As part of your treatment regimen, you were initially prescribed Aspirin at 325 mg daily, Clopidogrel at 75 mg daily, and intravenous Heparin, adjusted per protocol. Nitroglycerin was also provided as needed for chest pain relief.

Further management included the introduction of a Beta-Blocker (Metoprolol 50 mg twice daily), an ACE Inhibitor (Lisinopril 10 mg daily), and a Statin (40 mg daily) to manage your cardiovascular risk factors, including elevated LDL cholesterol noted in your lipid profile.

Throughout your stay, your condition consistently improved. By March 2, 2024, you reported feeling better with effective pain management, and your troponin levels showed a decreasing trend, indicating stabilization of myocardial injury. Subsequent ward rounds confirmed your progressively stable condition without complications, and preparations for your discharge were initiated.

At the time of discharge on March 6, 2024, you were in good condition with significant improvement noted. Comprehensive discharge instructions were provided, and you were educated on your medication regimen and lifestyle modifications, including adopting a heart-healthy diet, gradual reintroduction of physical activity, smoking cessation, and stress management techniques.

You were brought to the hospital after experiencing a fall, accompanied by a subsequent loss of consciousness. Upon your arrival, our team evaluated you thoroughly to determine the appropriate course of action.

Your condition was quickly assessed with a CT scan conducted on March 11, 2024. The results revealed a hemorrhagic stroke located in the right hemisphere of your brain, categorized as an intracerebral hemorrhage (diagnosis code I61.9). Due to the critical nature of this condition, a treatment regimen was promptly initiated to manage the symptoms and prevent further complications.

You were prescribed Mannitol, dosed at 0.25 g/kg and administered every 6 hours, to address cerebral edema. Additionally, antihypertensive medications were prescribed, tailored according to your blood pressure levels, to support your cardiovascular stability.

Laboratory tests conducted on March 11, 2024, indicated a slightly low hemoglobin count and elevated white blood cell levels. Additionally, your coagulation profile showed an elevated INR, which suggests a prolonged bleeding time requiring close monitoring.

Throughout your stay in the hospital, you exhibited symptoms consistent with a reduced level of consciousness, reflected in your steady Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 11, slight slurring of speech, and right-sided weakness. As noted in the medical evaluations on March 11 and 12, your neurological status remained critical, with persistent difficulties. Discussions were held with your family to inform them about your prognosis and the necessity for continued care.

Despite measures to ensure your safety and attempts to stabilize your condition, you remain in need of specialized care. Safety protocols have been put in place to protect you from further falls and complications.

We aim to have everything prepared for your anticipated discharge on March 20, 2024. In the meantime, please do not hesitate to reach out to us with any questions or concerns you might have regarding your treatment or discharge planning.

During your hospital stay, you underwent a comprehensive assessment, including a CT scan conducted on March 11, 2024. The imaging confirmed the presence of a hemorrhagic stroke in the right hemisphere of your brain, leading to an unspecified intracerebral hemorrhage diagnosis.

Despite our interventions, your neurological status showed limited improvement during your stay. Ward rounds recorded persistent issues, including right-sided weakness and ongoing slurred speech. Safety measures were enacted to prevent additional falls due to observed agitation and confusion. As of March 12, it was necessary to discuss your prognosis and continued care needs with your family, stressing that discharge was not yet deemed safe. Upon admission, it was noted that you exhibited a decreased level of consciousness with a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 11 and slurring of speech.

Subsequent laboratory tests revealed a complete blood count with slightly low hemoglobin and elevated white blood cell count. Your coagulation profile showed an elevated International Normalized Ratio (INR), indicating prolonged bleeding time.

In response to the intracerebral hemorrhage, medical interventions included the administration of Mannitol to manage cerebral edema, dosed at 0.25 g/kg every six hours, and antihypertensive medications were prescribed to regulate your blood pressure as needed. Despite these interventions, your neurological status did not show significant improvement initially, with your GCS remaining at 11 and exhibiting signs of agitation and confusion.

Throughout your hospitalization, your safety was a primary concern, and necessary precautions were taken to prevent further falls. Discussions were held with your family regarding your prognosis and ongoing care requirements due to persistent right-sided weakness and slurred speech.

As of March 20, 2024, your condition showed sufficient stabilization for a safe discharge. You are recommended to continue following up with your primary care physician and a neurologist for ongoing monitoring and management of your condition. _Expected Date of Discharge: 20th March 2024_

**Patient Information:**  
Name: ___  
Age: 65  
Gender: Female

**Reason for Admission:**  
The patient was admitted following a fall and subsequent loss of consciousness. Upon evaluation, a decreased level of consciousness was noted with a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) of 11 and slurred speech.

**Clinical Summary and Hospital Course:**  
Upon admission on the 10th of March 2024, the patient underwent routine investigations, including a computed tomography (CT) scan. The scan, conducted on the 11th of March 2024, identified a hemorrhagic stroke located in the right hemisphere. The diagnosis established was that of an unspecified intracerebral hemorrhage (ICD-10 Code: I61.9).

The laboratory investigations performed on the 11th of March 2024 revealed a slightly low hemoglobin level, elevated white blood cell (WBC) count, and an elevated International Normalized Ratio (INR), indicative of prolonged bleeding time. 

In response to the findings, the medical team initiated treatment with Mannitol at a dose of 0.25 g/kg, to be administered every 6 hours, targeting cerebral edema. Additionally, antihypertensives were prescribed and dosed in accordance with the patient’s blood pressure readings to manage hypertension. 

On the 11th of March, neurological assessments confirmed persistent right-sided weakness and slurred speech. GCS remained at 11. No significant improvement was noted in the evening assessment of the same day, as the patient exhibited agitation and confusion, necessitating the implementation of safety measures to prevent further falls.

By the morning of the 12th of March 2024, the patient's condition remained critical, with ongoing slurred speech and right-sided weakness. A discussion with the family ensued regarding prognosis and necessary ongoing care.

**Condition at Discharge:**  
As of the last clinical update available, the patient remains under careful observation, with no significant change reported in the neurological status. The patient continues to require comprehensive monitoring due to the critical nature of her condition. 

**Recommendations:**  
1. Given the complexity and severity of the identified condition, it is imperative that the patient undergoes regular monitoring and evaluation by her healthcare provider post-discharge.
2. The continuation of prescribed medications, including Mannitol and antihypertensives, should be adhered to meticulously.
3. Appropriate follow-up appointments need to be scheduled to assess recovery progress, adjust treatment plans as necessary, and ensure safety measures are maintained to prevent further incidents.

Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.

Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.

Dear Ms. ___,

I hope this letter finds you on a path to recovery. This document summarizes the details of your recent hospital stay from March 10, 2024, to your expected discharge on March 20, 2024, following an unfortunate fall and subsequent intracerebral hemorrhage.

You were admitted on March 10, 2024, following a fall that resulted in a loss of consciousness. Initial investigations included a CT scan carried out on March 11, 2024, which identified a hemorrhagic stroke in the right hemisphere of your brain.

Laboratory tests conducted on March 11 revealed a slightly low hemoglobin level and elevated white blood cell count. The coagulation profile indicated an elevated INR, suggesting a prolonged bleeding time. Consequently, treatment was initiated with Mannitol, administered at 0.25 g/kg every six hours, to address cerebral edema. Antihypertensive medication was also prescribed, with dosages adjusted according to your blood pressure readings.

During your stay, it was noted on March 11 that your neurological status showed persistent issues, with right-sided weakness and slurred speech. Although there was close monitoring, no significant improvement was detected in the evening of the same day, and you exhibited signs of agitation and confusion. Efforts such as safety measures were implemented to prevent any further falls. On March 12, discussions were initiated with your family regarding your prognosis and the essential ongoing care requirements, given the critical nature of your condition.

As of your discharge date, the critical condition persists, with continued manifestations of slurred speech and right-sided weakness. It is crucial to maintain close follow-up with your neurologist to monitor and manage your symptoms adequately. Additionally, adherence to prescribed antihypertensive medication will be essential to manage your blood pressure effectively.

Please ensure that you attend any follow-up appointments as scheduled and contact your healthcare provider should there be any deterioration in symptoms or any new concerns arise.

Wishing you a steady recovery and improved health.

<user>
The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

This is an example of discharge summary letter:
Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, following a fall and subsequent loss of consciousness. Your initial presentation included a decreased level of consciousness with a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 11, slurred speech, and the onset of neurological symptoms, warranting further investigation.

On March 11, 2024, a CT scan was conducted, revealing a hemorrhagic stroke resulting in an intracerebral hemorrhage in the right hemisphere. Concurrent laboratory tests displayed slight anemia with elevated white blood cell counts and an elevated International Normalized Ratio (INR), indicating prolonged bleeding time. 

In response to the findings, treatment commenced with the administration of Mannitol at a dosage of 0.25 g/kg every six hours to reduce cerebral edema. Additionally, antihypertensive medications were instituted to manage blood pressure, with doses adjusted according to your blood pressure readings.

Throughout your stay, you exhibited persistent right-sided weakness and slurred speech. Frequent evaluations were conducted, revealing no significant neurological improvements, with your GCS score remaining at 11. You also experienced episodes of agitation and confusion, necessitating close monitoring and the implementation of safety measures to prevent additional falls.

Discussions with your family regarding your prognosis and ongoing care needs took place, emphasizing the critical nature of your condition and the requirement for continued medical supervision. You remained hemodynamically stable without further decline in your neurological assessment until your eventual discharge on March 20, 2024.

At discharge, you still exhibit right-sided weakness and slurred speech. It is imperative to follow the outlined care plan upon returning home. You will need frequent follow-ups with your healthcare providers to monitor your progress and adjust your treatment plan as necessary. Continued management of hypertension and attention to potential coagulation issues are recommended.

Further neurological evaluations and rehabilitative therapy will likely be beneficial in optimizing your recovery. Please ensure that you attend all scheduled outpatient appointments for ongoing assessment and management. 

Should any concerns arise following your discharge, promptly contacting your primary care physician or seeking medical evaluation will be essential. An appointment with your general practitioner is advisable within the subsequent weeks to coordinate care and address any emerging needs or adjustments to your treatment regimen.

We wish you the best for your recovery.

Sincerely,
____

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, following a fall and subsequent loss of consciousness. Upon admission, it was noted that your level of consciousness had decreased, with a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score recorded at 11. Additionally, you exhibited slurred speech.

On March 11, 2024, a CT scan of your brain revealed a hemorrhagic stroke located in the right hemisphere. Consequently, a diagnosis of unspecified intracerebral hemorrhage (I61.9) was established. To manage cerebral edema, you were started on Mannitol at a dosage of 0.25 g/kg every six hours. Furthermore, antihypertensive medication was administered with dosages adjusted according to your blood pressure levels.

Your initial laboratory investigations, including a complete blood count (CBC), indicated that hemoglobin levels were slightly low, and white blood cell counts were elevated. The coagulation profile revealed an elevated INR, suggestive of a prolonged bleeding time.

Throughout your hospitalization, regular neurological examinations confirmed right-sided weakness and persistent slurred speech. Despite continued treatment, there was no significant improvement in your neurological status, and your GCS remained at 11. Agitation and confusion were noted during the evening assessment on March 11, 2024. Given these findings, you were not deemed safe for discharge at that juncture, necessitating continued monitoring and safety measures to prevent further falls.

On March 12, 2024, your condition remained critical with ongoing slurred speech and right-sided weakness. Discussions were held with your family regarding your prognosis and ongoing care needs.

At the time of discharge, your condition should be reassessed to ensure stability and safety for returning home or transferred to a rehabilitation facility as determined appropriate.

Your follow-up care plan will be important in monitoring your recovery and addressing any ongoing healthcare needs. Please make arrangements for continued medical care and attend follow-up appointments as recommended by your healthcare provider.

Wishing you a progressive recovery and improved health.

Sincerely,

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, following a fall and a subsequent loss of consciousness. Upon admission, you exhibited a decreased level of consciousness, and a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 11 was recorded, alongside slurring of speech.

The following day, March 11, 2024, a CT scan was conducted, which confirmed a diagnosis of hemorrhagic stroke (diagnosis code I61.9) affecting the right hemisphere, as indicated by an intracerebral hemorrhage. This diagnosis was followed by a comprehensive evaluation of your neurological function, which revealed persistent right-sided weakness and continued slurred speech. In light of these findings, it was determined that close monitoring was necessary, and you were not deemed safe for discharge.

Your laboratory results from the same day included a complete blood count (CBC), which showed slightly low hemoglobin levels and an elevated white blood cell (WBC) count. A coagulation profile indicated an elevated INR, suggesting prolonged bleeding time. In response to the cerebral edema detected, a mannitol regimen was initiated on March 11, administered at a dose of 0.25 g/kg every 6 hours. Additionally, antihypertensive medications were prescribed based on your blood pressure readings.

On the evening of March 11, despite treatment measures, your neurological status showed no significant improvement, with GCS remaining at 11. You showed signs of agitation and confusion, and appropriate safety measures were implemented to minimize the risk of further falls.

By the morning of March 12, your condition remained critical, with ongoing right-sided weakness and persistent slurred speech. Discussions were held with your family regarding your prognosis and the need for continued care. At that time, you were not safe for discharge.

You are expected to be discharged from the hospital on March 20, 2024, and it is crucial to continue with the prescribed management plan and follow-up care. Please ensure to attend all subsequent appointments and adhere to the treatment regime as advised by your healthcare team.

We trust that your ongoing care will be managed effectively, and recommend close observation by your healthcare providers. Should you or your family have any questions or require further information, please feel free to contact us.

Sincerely,  
___

The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

This is an example of discharge summary letter:
Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

Given the critical nature of your condition and the ongoing symptoms, a discussion was held with your family concerning your prognosis and the need for ongoing care and close monitoring.

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, following a fall that resulted in a subsequent loss of consciousness. On initial assessment, you exhibited a decreased level of consciousness with a Glasgow Coma Scale (GCS) score of 11. Slurring of speech was noted during the evaluation on the evening of your admission.

On March 11, 2024, a CT scan was performed, which identified a hemorrhagic stroke located in the right hemisphere of your brain. This finding confirmed the diagnosis of an intracerebral hemorrhage, unspecified, as documented in your records.

Considering the CT scan results, treatment with Mannitol was initiated at a dose of 0.25 g/kg every 6 hours to manage cerebral edema. Additionally, antihypertensive medication was administered as needed, based on your blood pressure readings, to mitigate further risks.

**Discharge Summary Letter**

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, due to concerns of chest pain (CP) and shortness of breath (SoB), as indicated in the emergency communication. Upon arrival, an electrocardiogram (ECG) was performed, revealing significant ST elevation in leads v5-6. This initial assessment led to the clinical diagnosis of a ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI), specifically involving the left main coronary artery on March 11, 2024.

During your stay, your condition was closely monitored and managed per the established cardiac care protocols. ECG findings persisted, and the local practice guidelines suggested you might benefit from a percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI). However, despite efforts, we were unable to contact the cath lab, and our cardiology team was not responsive at the bleep. Consequently, we prioritized medication management to stabilize your condition. 

You have been administered MONAC, which included morphine for pain relief at a dose of 2-4 mg as needed, oxygen therapy of 2-4 L/min if your oxygen saturation dropped below 90%, nitroglycerin every five minutes for a maximum of three doses if chest pain persisted, and a single dose of 324 mg of aspirin which was advised to be chewed for rapid absorption. Serial measurements of Troponin T were conducted and the levels were significantly elevated, recorded at 30 and 29 respectively, confirming myocardial injury.

On the evening of March 12, 2024, after reporting resolution of your chest pain, you requested to be discharged. Kindly note that continued outpatient follow-up with a cardiologist is strongly recommended to ensure your cardiac health is optimally managed. Safe practices include adherence to the prescribed medication regimen and seeking immediate attention should any symptoms recur.

Thank you for entrusting your care with us. We wish you a swift recovery and a healthy future.

Yours sincerely,

Dr. ___  
Cardiology Department

Dear Mr. ___,

We hope this letter finds you in good health. We are writing to provide a summary of your recent hospital stay and your current condition, as well as to outline recommendations for your follow-up care.

Your admission to the hospital occurred on March 10, 2024, following an acute presentation of chest pain and shortness of breath. Initial assessments, including an Electrocardiogram (ECG), revealed ST elevation in leads V5-6, indicative of an acute myocardial infarction. This clinical presentation led to your admission under the diagnosis of ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery (ICD-10 code I21.01).

Over the course of your hospitalization, a series of interventions were undertaken in accordance with the standard management of myocardial infarctions. You received appropriate medical therapy, including administration of Morphine for pain management, Oxygen supplementation to maintain oxygen saturation levels, Nitroglycerin for chest pain relief, and a single dose of Aspirin for its antiplatelet effects. Despite attempts to arrange Percutaneous Coronary Intervention (PCI) as per local hospital guidelines, communication barriers precluded timely access to catheterization services.

Cardiac enzymes were assessed, with Troponin T levels recorded at 30 and 29 on March 11, 2024. These elevated biomarkers confirmed myocardial injury consistent with your clinical diagnosis.

Throughout your stay, your condition was closely monitored, and by the night of March 12, 2024, you reported a resolution of chest pain. In light of your improved clinical condition and upon your request for discharge, you have been deemed stable for discharge with guidance for further management.

For your ongoing care, we recommend scheduled follow-up with your primary care physician or cardiologist to monitor your cardiovascular health. It is essential to adhere to prescribed medications and to notify your healthcare provider of any recurrence of symptoms such as chest pain or shortness of breath. Lifestyle modifications, including adherence to a heart-healthy diet, regular physical activity, and smoking cessation, are strongly advised to support your recovery and prevent future cardiac events.

Thank you for entrusting us with your care. Please do not hesitate to contact us or your healthcare provider should you have further questions or concerns.

Wishing you a smooth recovery and continued health.

Dear ___,  

We are writing this letter in regard to your recent hospitalization at [Hospital Name], which spanned from March 10, 2024, to your anticipated discharge on March 12, 2024. 

On March 10, 2024, you were admitted to our facility with complaints of chest pain and shortness of breath. An initial electrocardiogram (ECG) demonstrated significant ST elevation in leads V5-6, which prompted immediate admission for further cardiac assessment due to the suspicion of a ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI).

On the following day, March 11, further cardiac evaluations were undertaken. Serial ECG monitoring confirmed persistent ST elevation, and blood tests exhibited elevated Troponin T levels, with results recorded as 30 ng/L and 29 ng/L, indicating cardiac ischemia. You were consequently diagnosed with an ST elevation myocardial infarction involving the left main coronary artery.

During your stay, you received pharmacological treatment following local hospital protocols, including Morphine as needed for pain management, Oxygen supplementation if oxygen saturation fell below 90%, and Nitroglycerin every five minutes for three doses if chest pain persisted. A single dose of Aspirin was administered, chewed, at a dosage of 324 mg to aid in antiplatelet therapy.

Despite efforts to follow guidelines, we were faced with logistical challenges as immediate contact with the catheterization lab was unattainable, and communication with the cardiology team was restricted. Despite these circumstances, medical management remained the primary focus.

Dear ___,

This letter serves as a summary of your recent hospitalization at our facility from March 10, 2024, to March 12, 2024. During this time, you were admitted due to chest pain and shortness of breath, accompanied by ST elevation observed in leads V5 and V6 on your ECG. You arrived via ambulance and were admitted for suspected ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery, as confirmed by your diagnosis on March 11, 2024.

Upon admission, immediate medical intervention was initiated. An ECG conducted on March 11, 2024, repeatedly showed ST elevation, warranting the administration of the standardized MONAC protocol, which included morphine, oxygen, nitroglycerin, aspirin, and clopidogrel. Morphine was provided at 2-4 mg doses as needed for pain relief. You received oxygen therapy at 2-4 L/min if your oxygen saturation dropped below 90%. Nitroglycerin, 0.4 mg, was administered every 5 minutes for up to three doses should pain persist, and a single dose of 324 mg aspirin was given, to be chewed, in accordance with the emergency management of myocardial infarction.

Laboratory results from March 11, 2024, indicated elevated levels of Troponin T at 30 and 29, corroborating the diagnosis of myocardial infarction.

During your stay, attempts to perform a primary coronary angioplasty per local hospital guidelines were hampered due to an inability to contact the catheterization lab, and there was no response to the cardiology team's alert. Consequently, management focused on optimal medication regimen and monitoring.

Throughout observation, your condition stabilized. On the night of March 12, 2024, you reported a resolution of chest pain and requested discharge. At discharge, you appeared stable, with no further chest pain or significant issues reported.

Post-discharge, it is imperative you adhere to the prescribed medication regimen and attend scheduled follow-up appointments with your cardiologist to monitor your cardiac health closely. Please seek immediate medical attention if chest pain or any other concerning symptoms reoccur.

Please feel free to contact our office or your healthcare provider if you have any questions or require further assistance regarding your ongoing treatment plan.

We wish you a smooth recovery and thank you for entrusting us with your care.

Dr. ___

Dear [Patient's Name],

I hope this letter finds you well after your recent hospital stay. As your treating physician, I am writing to provide a summary of your hospitalization and the care you received.

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, after presenting with chest pain and shortness of breath. An electrocardiogram (ECG) conducted upon your arrival revealed significant ST elevation in leads v5-6, prompting a preliminary diagnosis of ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery. Given these findings, admission was deemed necessary for further management.

During your stay on March 11, 2024, additional diagnostic tests confirmed the presence of a myocardial infarction. This diagnosis was supported by elevated levels of Troponin T, with results showing 30 and subsequently 29. These laboratory findings reinforced the clinical picture of a myocardial infarction.

In accordance with the standard protocol for managing STEMI, you received treatment with the MONAC protocol, incorporating Morphine (2-4 mg as needed for pain), Oxygen (2-4 L/min if oxygen saturation fell below 90%), Nitroglycerin (0.4 mg every 5 minutes for up to three doses if pain persisted), and a single dose of Aspirin (324 mg, chewed).

Efforts to perform percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) were hampered by an inability to contact the catheterization laboratory and a lack of response from the cardiology team. Despite these constraints, you were closely monitored, and your pain was managed effectively. A note from the night of March 11, 2024, indicates that ECG findings showed persistent ST elevation; however, your condition remained stable.

By the night of March 12, 2024, your chest pain had resolved satisfactorily, and at your request, preparations for your discharge were initiated. At the time of discharge, you reported an absence of symptoms, expressing your desire to continue recuperating at home.

For your continued recovery, it is essential to adhere to the following recommendations:
- Please schedule an appointment with your cardiologist for follow-up evaluation and management to ensure continued cardiac health.
- Ensure compliance with any medication regimen prescribed to you, particularly those aimed at managing heart conditions or risk factors.
- Follow a heart-healthy lifestyle, including dietary modifications and regular exercise, as advised by your healthcare provider.

Thank you for entrusting us with your care. Should you have any questions or require further assistance, please do not hesitate to contact our clinical team.

Re: Discharge Summary  

I hope this letter finds you well. This letter serves as the summary of your recent hospitalization from March 10, 2024, to March 12, 2024, at [Hospital Name]. Below is a detailed account of your stay and the treatment you received.  

On March 10, 2024, you presented at our facility with complaints of chest pain and shortness of breath. An ambulance transported you to the hospital where an initial Electrocardiogram (ECG) revealed ST elevation in leads V5 and V6. Given these findings and your symptoms, you were admitted with a preliminary diagnosis of ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery.  

Upon admission, a series of therapeutic interventions were initiated following local hospital guidelines. You received MONAC treatment, which included Morphine for pain management at a dose of 2-4 mg as needed. Oxygen therapy was administered at 2-4 L/min if your oxygen saturation fell below 90%. Nitroglycerin was provided at a dose of 0.4 mg every five minutes for up to three doses if pain persisted, along with a single dose of 324 mg of chewed Aspirin.  

Laboratory tests conducted on March 11, 2024, indicated elevated Troponin T levels of 30 and 29, confirming the diagnosis of myocardial infarction. Efforts to contact the cardiac catheterization lab for a possible percutaneous coronary intervention (PCA) were unsuccessful, and persistent ST elevation was noted in subsequent ECGs. Efforts to reach the cardiology team were also unresponsive at the time, as mentioned in the attending physician's notes.

On the evening of March 12, 2024, you indicated that your chest pain had resolved and requested discharge. Considering your improved condition, it was deemed appropriate to discharge you under the condition that you follow up with your cardiologist for further outpatient management.

At discharge, your condition appeared stable with the resolution of acute symptoms. It is imperative that you adhere to the following recommendations for your continued care:

1. Schedule a follow-up appointment with your cardiologist at the earliest convenience to discuss further cardiac assessments and interventions.
2. Maintain any prescribed medications as per discharge instructions.
3. Seek immediate medical attention should you experience recurrent or worsening symptoms, including chest pain, shortness of breath, or palpitations.

We appreciate your cooperation and understanding during your stay, and we are committed to ensuring you receive the best ongoing care. Should you have any questions or require additional clarification regarding your treatment plan, please do not hesitate to contact our office directly.

<user>
The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

This is an example of discharge summary letter:
Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, presenting with chest pain (CP) and shortness of breath (SoB). An electrocardiogram (ECG) carried out shortly after your arrival indicated ST elevation in leads V5-6, suggestive of an acute coronary syndrome. Based on your clinical presentation and the ECG findings, it was determined that you were experiencing a ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery.

During your hospital stay, you were administered the MONAC (Morphine, Oxygen, Nitroglycerin, Aspirin, and Clopidogrel) protocol, as part of standard medical therapy for STEMI. The medications included Morphine (2-4 mg, as needed for pain), Oxygen (2-4 L/min if oxygen saturation was less than 90%), Nitroglycerin (0.4 mg every 5 minutes for up to 3 doses if pain persisted), and a single dose of Aspirin (324 mg, chewed). Despite efforts, the local hospital guidelines suggested percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI), but there were logistical challenges in contacting the catheterization lab and the cardiology team initially did not respond to pages. 

Laboratory tests to assess cardiac enzymes showed elevated levels of Troponin T (30 and 29 respectively), confirming myocardial injury.

Throughout your stay, you were closely monitored, and the persistent ST elevation noted on ECG necessitated careful observation and continued medical management. On March 12, 2024, you expressed the desire to be discharged, as your chest pain had resolved, suggesting a stabilization of your condition.

At discharge, your immediate condition appears stable, with resolved chest pain and no acute distress. It is advisable to follow up regularly with your primary care physician and cardiologist to monitor your cardiac health and continue necessary treatment. It is crucial to adhere to the prescribed medication regimen and report any recurrence of chest pain or new symptoms promptly. Further assessment and possibly a referral for PCI could be considered during your ongoing outpatient care.

Please schedule an appointment with your cardiology outpatient clinic to ensure comprehensive follow-up and management of your cardiac condition.

Sincerely,

___

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, after presenting with chest pain and shortness of breath. An electrocardiogram (ECG) conducted upon arrival revealed ST elevation in leads v5-6, indicative of an acute ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery. These findings necessitated immediate medical intervention according to local hospital guidelines.

Upon admission, you received the MONAC protocol, which included morphine, oxygen, nitroglycerin, aspirin, and other necessary treatments to stabilize your condition. These interventions aimed to alleviate pain and improve cardiac oxygenation. Despite attempts to proceed with percutaneous coronary intervention (PCI) as per protocol, difficulties arose in contacting the catheterization lab, and the cardiology team was unable to be reached at the time. Consequently, management followed a conservative approach while ensuring ongoing monitoring of your condition.

Laboratory tests conducted on March 11th showed elevated levels of Troponin T, with results of 30 and 29, confirming myocardial injury. Despite persistent ST elevation monitored through subsequent ECGs, your condition showed clinical improvement with the resolution of chest pain, as noted in the ward rounds.

On the night of March 12th, you expressed a wish to be discharged due to the complete resolution of chest pain. At the time of discharge, you appeared stable with no active ischemic discomfort. As part of your ongoing care, it is important to adhere to prescribed medications and monitor for any recurrence of symptoms. Follow-up appointments are essential to facilitate continuous evaluation and adjustment of your treatment plan.

Please ensure to maintain communication with your primary care physician for further management of your cardiac health and attend scheduled cardiology outpatient reviews. Should any concerns or symptoms arise prior to your follow-up visit, do not hesitate to seek immediate medical attention.

We appreciate your cooperation during your stay and wish you a smooth recovery.

Dear ___,

You were admitted to the hospital on March 10, 2024, due to chest pain (CP) and shortness of breath (SoB), as recorded by emergency medical personnel upon your arrival. Your initial electrocardiogram (ECG) indicated an ST segment elevation in leads v5-6, consistent with an ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI), specifically involving the left main coronary artery. In accordance with these findings, you were admitted for further evaluation and management.

On March 11, 2024, a follow-up ECG corroborated the initial findings of persistent ST segment elevation. You received immediate medical management, which included the administration of the MONAC protocol (Morphine, Oxygen, Nitroglycerin, Aspirin, and Clopidogrel). Your laboratory results on the same day showed elevated Troponin T levels, with values of 30 and 29, confirming myocardial injury.

During your hospital stay, it was noted that local guidelines suggested Percutaneous Coronary Intervention (PCI), but there were challenges in obtaining access to the cardiac catheterization laboratory, and communication with the cardiology team was delayed. Despite these logistical barriers, your chest pain eventually resolved, and you expressed a desire to be discharged.

At the time of your discharge on March 12, 2024, your symptoms had significantly improved, and you reported no further chest pain. Your heart condition is now stable, and you have been provided with instructions on continued medication, lifestyle modifications, and follow-up appointments.

You should adhere to your prescribed medication regimen vigilantly. This includes continuing Morphine as needed for any residual pain, maintaining oxygen therapy if your saturation levels fall below 90%, administering Nitroglycerin sublingually every five minutes for up to three doses if chest pain persists, and Aspirin for its antiplatelet effects as directed.

It is imperative that you follow up with your primary care physician and cardiologist as soon as possible to reassess your cardiac health and evaluate further treatment strategy. You will also need regular monitoring of your cardiovascular status to prevent any recurrence of symptoms.

Please feel free to reach out to your healthcare provider if you experience any worsening of symptoms or have any questions regarding your treatment. We sincerely wish you a swift and full recovery.

Sincerely,

___

The letter is written to a patient when leaving hospital after a stay for the review. It should be prose and not contain bullet points.
Include summary of stay including, if applicable, key issues, interventions, procedures, and treatments.
Include summary of condition at discharge.
Include recommendations for follow-up plans and ongoing treatment.
Write the text in the style of a discharge letter using medical terminology. Proceed chronologically, write factually, objectively and concretely, formally, professionally, politely, respectfully. Avoid generalizations, assumptions, verbose and cumbersome formulations, and redundancy. Do not invent or presume anything. Generally, formulate in the imperfect tense, reported speech from individuals (e.g., from patients) in subjunctive I, and medical assessments and diagnoses in the present tense.
Minimize hallucinations. Do not generate recommendations on your own or add anything. Write nothing about topics for which there is no information basis from the EHR.
Don't include all private information, such as ID and date of birth. Use ___ to represent names of patients and medical professionals.

Dear ___,

You came into hospital because you were getting more short of breath and your ankles were swollen.

A heart scan, called an echocardiogram, showed that your heart was not pumping as well as it should.

We started you on water tablets called Furosemide. They will stop fluid collecting in your legs and lungs.

Please take 2 Furosemide tablets (2 x 40 mg = 80 mg) each morning and weigh yourself each morning.

If your weight drops by more than 2 kg over the week, reduce the dose to 1 tablet per day.

We have asked your GP practice to see you in about 2 weeks' time. They will take a blood test to check your kidney function.

We will see you in 6 weeks' time in the Cardiology Outpatient Clinic.

You will receive an appointment letter in the post.

Sincerely,
____

Dear ___,

You were admitted to our hospital on March 10, 2024, following an episode of chest pain (CP) and shortness of breath (SoB). Initial evaluation through an Electrocardiogram (ECG) indicated ST elevation in leads v5-6, suggestive of an ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery. In accordance with local hospital guidelines, a percutaneous coronary intervention (PCA) was recommended, but there were challenges in reaching the catheterization lab and contacting the cardiology team.

On March 11, 2024, the diagnosis of STEMI was confirmed. Laboratory investigations revealed elevated Troponin T levels, with results of 30 and 29. As part of your management, you received the MONAC treatment protocol, which included the administration of Morphine (2-4 mg) as needed for pain, Oxygen therapy (2-4 L/min) if oxygen saturation fell below 90%, Nitroglycerin (0.4 mg) every 5 minutes for up to three doses if pain persisted, and a single chewed dose of Aspirin (324 mg).

Throughout your hospital stay, despite persistent ST elevation observed in subsequent ECG evaluations, the clinical condition showed improvement. By the evening of March 12, 2024, you reported a resolution of chest pain and requested discharge.

At the time of discharge, your condition appears stable, and your symptoms have resolved. We recommend that you follow up with your primary care physician and the cardiology team for continued monitoring and to discuss any further interventions that might be required. It is crucial to adhere to your prescribed medication regimen and attend all scheduled follow-up appointments. Should you experience a recurrence of chest pain or other concerning symptoms, please seek immediate medical attention.

We wish you a speedy recovery and good health. Please feel free to reach out if you have any queries or require additional assistance.

Sincerely,

Dr. ___  
Resident Physician

You were admitted to the hospital on the 10th of March, 2024, due to symptoms of chest pain and shortness of breath, observed with ST elevation in leads v5-6 on the ECG, indicating a potential cardiac event. Upon admission, an ECG confirmed the presence of a ST elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) involving the left main coronary artery, coded as I2101. This condition required immediate medical intervention.

During your hospital stay, you underwent several investigations. Blood tests on the 11th of March showed elevated levels of Troponin T, with results recorded as 30 and 29, confirming myocardial infarction. Treatment involved administration of medications in accordance with our MONAC protocol. You received Morphine for pain management as needed, Oxygen therapy was provided if oxygen saturation fell below 90%, and Nitroglycerin was available every 5 minutes up to three doses for persistent chest pain. Aspirin, 324 mg, was administered as a single, chewed dose to help prevent further clot formation.

Unfortunately, due to logistical issues, percutaneous coronary intervention (PCA), a recommended procedure, could not be performed as the catheterization laboratory was inaccessible and the cardiology team was unresponsive. Despite these challenges, you received comprehensive care following the local hospital guidelines for STEMI management.

By the night of the 12th March, as documented in the medical notes, you expressed a desire to be discharged as your chest pain had resolved. At the time of discharge, your condition was stable, and you had responded well to the medical management provided.

It is crucial to follow up with your general practitioner (GP) promptly to ensure continued recovery and prevent any further complications. Additional evaluations with a cardiologist may also be required to assess long-term management strategies. Please strictly adhere to any prescribed medications and attend all scheduled follow-up appointments.

Wishing you a smooth and swift recovery.
